Linear regression ^EL8eTNiv

Maybe the simplest possible leaning algorithm for a supervised learning regression problem.
 ^PDU43WB5

Example: lets say you want to predict or estimate the prices of houses. ^RMjPnfOq

In supervised learning you have a Training set (such as the dataset), and you feed this to a learning algorithm, whose job is to output a function to make predictions, which we call a hypothesis. ^ErvOiBC9

Training set ^tMUiwz8K

Learning algorithm ^SxmoA8Os

How do you represent H? ^QnlqnRLD

in linear regression and in this case it will be: ^tOasrAcM

In this example we have just one input feature X, if you have
multiple input features (like, number of bedrooms), more generally
we can have: ^zUsjXl7Z

Some notations:
θ = "parameters"
m = #training examples
     (#rows in table above)
x = "inputs"/features
y = "output"/target variable
(x,y) = training example
 ^WmZi2CMZ

n = #features ^CXOv7yPN

training example ^pq5fFD4w

How do you choose the parameters?
Choose θ st.(such that) h(x) ~ y for the training examples. We
want to minimize θ. So we define the cost function J(θ) to be
minimized. ^Qz4FLfMn

J(θ) = ^O7jx5Bz7

Gradient Descent ^AXL3gsWp

Algorithm to find a value of θ that minimizes J(θ).  ^QcYtOciA

Start with some θ (say θ = 0)
Keep changing θ to resolve J(θ) ^2O0UVcQn

Your objective is to minimize J(θ),
so you will start at some point
and look around all 360 degrees
and see in what direction you need
to take a step to go downhill as 
quickly as possible.
And then repeat.
Until you get a hopefully local optimum. ^BwmGE1QE

In this example the training set is fixed.
One step of the gradient descent: ^voYVAJV6

One more notation:
a := a+1 is an assignment of value
"a = b" is a statment ^Ssr6sX3L

learning
rate ^Kwm3f6PP

(j=0,1,2,...,n) ^9bmQYdPx

partial derivative
of J(θ) ^5j62ZhjF

the derivative of a function
defines the direction of steepest descent. ^FYs4pLPf

The definition when using all of the training examples is: ^e77i3uPg

Repeat until convergence: ^MA8Ce8bq

(for j=0,1,...,n) ^r5hl7aa8

If J(θ) is declared as the sum of squared terms, then it turns out to be a quadratic function, so it will look like a bowl. So it doesn't have a local optimal, just a global optimal. ^HgPpt5OL

Choice of Learning rate(α):
If you set α to be too large you can overshoot, the steps you take can be too large and you can run past the minimum. If you see J(θ) increasing its a sign that the α is too big.
If you set it to be too small then you need a lot of iterations and the algorithm will be slow.
In practice: try out multiple values of α and try then in a exponential scale. ^9nSQhqvR

Batch gradient descent ^4HzPOKHx

Batch reefers that you think of all examples as one batch of data. The disadvantage is that in order to make one update on the parameters, you need to scan through the entire database and sum, making it slow for big amounts of data. And if you need hundreds of iterations to converge, you will be scanning through your entire dataset hundreds of times.  ^ZUqfAMYd

Other name for this algorithm is ^nQlNFvSs

An alternative is ^9CbUEBj1

Stochastic gradient descent ^4I5MN0nY

Repeat {
    For i=1 to m {
        


    } 
} ^ITHCdgAY

Instead of scanning through all the dataset before you update the parameters, in the inner loop of the algorithm you loop through i=1 to m, using the derivative of just one single example. ^qgxSbHfA

update for every j ^eNn4Uemp

0 ^fqCanxBr

1 ^NauzSJur

θ ^A1ZQjkGX

θ ^YYNfE1TA

Each iteration you will probably improve the parameters but not going into the most direct downhill. It takes a more noisy path but on average its headed to the global minimum.
It will never quite converge but it makes a faster progress. ^fZQglya6

In practice, we rarely use batch gradient descent to big datasets because its really slow, you can use a mini-batch, but usually you use stochastic and reduce the learning rate over time. ^IFcWYhqS

There is a way that works only for linear regression, with it you can take one step to the global optimum without needing to use an iterative algorithm, it is ^lRBegySf

Normal equation ^5eHv0NVr

How to get to this equation in
